Patient Letter Evaluation Rubric

SECTION 1: LIKERT RATING SCALES

1. Accuracy — Are the medical facts correct and consistent with the original note?

Score 1 2 3 4 5
Label Major errors Multiple errors One error Minor imprecision Fully accurate
Definition Major factual errors that fundamentally misrepresent the patient's condition or treatment Multiple clinically relevant errors that could mislead the patient One clinically relevant error OR two or more minor imprecisions One minor factual imprecision that does not affect clinical meaning All medical facts are correct and consistent with the original note

2. Completeness — Is all clinically important information included?

Score 1 2 3 4 5
Label Critical gaps Major gaps One important gap Minor gap Fully complete
Definition Omits critical information such as the diagnosis or treatment plan Two or more clinically important elements are missing One clinically important element is missing (e.g. staging) One minor piece of information is missing but core clinical content is intact All clinically important information is represented

3. Comprehensible — Could a patient with an 8th-grade reading level understand this?

Score 1 2 3 4 5
Label Clinical language Largely unclear Partially clear Mostly clear Entirely clear
Definition Written in clinical language. A patient without medical training would not understand it Multiple sections require medical knowledge to understand Some sections use jargon or complex language that would confuse a lay reader One or two phrases may need slight clarification but overall meaning is accessible A patient with an 8th-grade reading level could understand every sentence

4. Useful — Would this summary help the patient understand their situation and next steps?

Score 1 2 3 4 5
Label Not useful Limited use Somewhat useful Useful Highly useful
Definition Patient would gain little actionable understanding from this summary Information is present but poorly organized Provides general information but patient would still have significant questions Provides relevant information but one actionable element is vague or missing Patient would clearly understand their diagnosis, what happens next, and what actions to take

SECTION 2: BINARY AND CATEGORICAL QUESTIONS

5. Hallucination — Does this summary contain any information that is NOT present in or directly inferable from the original clinical note?

  • Yes / No
  • If Yes, please underline the specific content in the summary letter and briefly describe below.

6. Deployment Readiness — Would you be comfortable sending this summary to a patient without further editing?

  • Ready to send as-is
  • Minor edits needed (1–2 small changes)
  • Major revision required (substantial rewriting needed)

Pancreatic Cancer — Sample 1

Original Clinical Note

SUBJECTIVE:    This is a very pleasant 47-year-old African-American gentleman with a diagnosis of locally advanced adenocarcinoma of the pancreas. He presented with several months of vague abdominal discomfort and had some gross hematuria in September. He went to ***** Hospital and a CT scan was done which revealed a pancreatic mass. His hematuria was felt to be secondary to renal lithiasis. This problem resolved.    He was noted to have biliary obstruction and underwent an ERCP with an EUS at ***** on October 24. A plastic stent was placed. Fine-needle aspiration of the mass revealed adenocarcinoma.    When I initially saw him, he had lost 43 pounds and was having a lot of pain. We elected to start gemcitabine and Abraxane. He's completed 6 full cycles. At the end of 2 cycles, his CT scan suggested progression. However, since he was doing so well clinically, we felt that this represented pseudo-progression.    His 5th cycle treatment was interrupted because of biliary ductal obstruction. He had a plastic stent was replaced with an uncovered metal stent. Apparently there was a lot of pus and material that drained at the time of that procedure and he was placed on IV and oral antibiotics. Since the procedure, he has had intermittent abdominal discomfort, usually at night, requiring oxycodone.     He feels that his appetite has returned. He's gained kilogram last month. He notices some numbness and tingling in his hands but it doesn't interfere with his function. He is having regular bowel movements.    He would like to meet with psycho oncology as he is having some difficulty coping with his disease and with some social and economic issues.    His girlfriend gave birth to a baby girl on April 27. Letter A

Dear Patient,

I want to explain your recent care in a clear and supportive way.

You have adenocarcinoma of the pancreas, which is a type of pancreatic cancer. Your cancer is described as locally advanced, meaning it has grown into nearby blood vessels and tissues and cannot be removed with surgery at this time.

When you were first diagnosed, you had a blockage in the bile duct (a tube that carries bile from the liver). You had a procedure called ERCP (a procedure to open blocked bile ducts), and a stent (a small tube) was placed to help bile flow normally. A biopsy confirmed the diagnosis of pancreatic cancer.

You were treated with chemotherapy using gemcitabine and Abraxane (medicines that help kill cancer cells) and completed six cycles. During treatment, one scan suggested possible growth of the tumor, but because you were feeling better, your doctors believed this may have been “pseudo-progression,” which means the tumor may look larger on scans due to treatment effects rather than true growth.

You also had a repeat procedure to replace your bile duct stent after it became blocked. Infection and buildup were found and treated with antibiotics. Since then, your appetite has improved, and you have gained some weight.

You report some symptoms, including:

Mild numbness and tingling in your hands Occasional abdominal discomfort Emotional stress related to your illness

Your recent imaging shows the pancreatic tumor is stable, with no clear spread in the abdomen. There are some small lung spots that need monitoring.

Next steps:

Take a break from chemotherapy (called surveillance, or close monitoring) Return for follow-up in about 2 months Continue managing symptoms and consider support services like psycho-oncology

Please remember, your care team is here to support both your physical and emotional health. Reach out with any concerns or questions.

With care, Your Care Team

Letter B

Dear Patient,

I hope this letter finds you well. I wanted to share some important information about your health and the next steps in your care.

Diagnosis and Stage: You have a type of cancer called locally advanced adenocarcinoma of the pancreas. This means the cancer started in your pancreas and has grown quite large.

Treatment Plan: You have been receiving two medicines called gemcitabine and Abraxane. You have finished six full treatments. Recently, you had a small tube called a stent placed in your bile duct to help with blockage.

Key Test Results: The latest scans show that the tumor in your pancreas has gotten a little bigger. However, there are no signs of the cancer spreading to other parts of your body. Some small spots were found in your lungs, but they are not causing any problems right now.

Next Steps: We think it’s okay for you to take a break from chemotherapy. We will keep watching you closely. You will come back to see us in two months for another check-up.

What to Watch For: Please let us know if you have any new pain, changes in how you feel, or any other concerns. It’s good to know that your appetite has returned and you’ve gained some weight. Keep an eye out for any new symptoms.

Additional Support: You mentioned wanting to talk to someone about coping with your illness. We can set up an appointment with a psychologist who specializes in helping people with cancer.

If you have any questions or need more information, please talk to your care team. We are here to support you.

Best wishes, [Your Name] Medical Communication Specialist

Letter C

Dear Patient, We hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.

Why did you come to the clinic?

You came in for a follow-up visit regarding your pancreatic cancer.

What's new or changed since your last visit?

There are new nodules in your lungs that might be cancer spread. Your pancreatic tumor has stayed about the same size. You've gained some weight, which is good. You still have some nighttime stomach pain that needs oxycodone.

What treatment or medication changes were made?

You asked for and were given a break from chemotherapy because you've had the best response possible from the treatment.

What is the plan going forward?

We will watch your condition closely without giving you any more chemotherapy for now. You will come back to the clinic in 2 months for a follow-up visit.

Thank you for trusting us with your care.

Please feel free to contact us if you have any questions.

Sincerely, Your Care Team

Letter A

Dear Patient,

I want to clearly explain your current health and care plan.

You have adenocarcinoma of the pancreas, which is a type of pancreatic cancer. You had surgery in the past (called a Whipple procedure, an operation to remove part of the pancreas and nearby organs). Now, the cancer has spread to other areas, including the liver, which means it is metastatic disease.

After surgery, you received chemotherapy called FOLFIRINOX (a combination of cancer-fighting medicines). At first, your tumor markers (blood tests that help track cancer, like CA 19-9) improved, but later scans showed that the cancer was growing again.

Because of this, your treatment was changed to a second-line chemotherapy called gemcitabine and Abraxane (nab-paclitaxel). You are currently continuing this treatment, and your care team feels it is safe to proceed at this time.

You have had some side effects from treatment. These include:

Numbness (neuropathy) in your hands and feet, which means nerve irritation Fatigue, or low energy Occasional fevers after treatment

Your nausea and vomiting have improved with medications. You are also taking pancreatic enzymes (Creon), which help your body digest food better since the pancreas is not working normally.

Next steps:

Continue your current chemotherapy treatment Have a CT scan after completing this treatment cycle to check how the cancer is responding Continue medications to control symptoms like nausea and digestion Monitor side effects, especially numbness, which may require treatment changes if it worsens

Your care team will continue to follow you closely. Please contact them if you have new symptoms, worsening side effects, or any concerns.

With care and support, Your Care Team

Letter B

Dear Mr. *,

Thank you for coming to see us today. Here is a summary of your visit:

Diagnosis and Stage: You have a type of cancer called pancreatic adenocarcinoma. The cancer has spread to other parts of your body, which we call metastatic disease.

Treatment Plan: You are now receiving a new treatment called Gemcitabine plus Abraxane. This treatment helps control the cancer. If you start to feel more numbness in your hands and feet, we might need to adjust the dose of one of these medicines.

Key Test Results: We checked your blood and found that your levels of a protein called CA 19-9 are high. This protein can show how well the treatment is working. Your levels have gone down, which is good news.

Next Steps: You will come back to see us in two weeks. We will do another CT scan after you finish this round of treatment.

What to Watch For: Please watch for any new symptoms like pain, fever, or feeling very tired. If you notice anything new or different, please call us right away.

Medications: You are taking several medications to help with pain, nausea, and other symptoms. Please keep taking them as directed.

Travel Plans: It’s great that you’re planning to visit your sister and brother-in-law. Make sure to take all your medications with you and stay in touch with us if you have any concerns.

If you have any questions or need more information, please talk to your care team. We are here to support you.

Warm regards,

Letter A

Dear Patient,

I want to explain your current health situation in a clear and caring way.

You have metastatic pancreatic cancer, which means cancer that started in the pancreas has spread to other parts of the body. Your cancer was first diagnosed in 2018.

You have received several treatments over time. These included different types of chemotherapy (medicines that kill cancer cells) such as FOLFIRINOX, FOLFOX, gemcitabine with nab-paclitaxel, and most recently a combination of 5-FU and nanoliposomal irinotecan (a newer form of chemotherapy). Some treatments worked for a time, but the cancer has continued to grow or spread.

Recent scans show a tumor in the tail of the pancreas that has grown into nearby organs like the spleen and stomach. There are also signs that the cancer has spread to the liver and the lining of the abdomen (called the peritoneum).

You also had a gastric outlet obstruction, which means the tumor was blocking food from leaving the stomach. A stent (a small tube placed to keep a passage open) was placed to help food pass through more easily. Since then, you have been able to eat better, although your appetite is still low.

You are currently experiencing symptoms such as fatigue, some abdominal pain, and low energy. Blood tests also showed anemia (low red blood cells), which was treated.

Treatment plan and next steps:

Continue one more cycle of your current chemotherapy, if your labs allow Repeat CT scans soon to check how the cancer is responding Consider clinical trials (research studies testing new treatments) Focus on comfort and quality of life, including symptom control Discuss whether to shift toward supportive care (care focused on comfort) or hospice in the future

Your care team will continue to support you and help guide these decisions. Please talk with your doctors about your goals, symptoms, and any questions you have.

With care and respect, Your Care Team

Letter A

Dear Patient,

I am writing to summarize your recent visit and care plan.

You have adenocarcinoma of the pancreas, which is a type of pancreatic cancer. At this time, your cancer is considered metastatic, meaning it has spread outside the pancreas. Earlier scans suggested a possible spot in the liver, but your most recent imaging shows no clear signs of cancer spread.

You previously received two types of chemotherapy. First, you were treated with FOLFIRINOX. After that, you received gemcitabine and Abraxane (chemotherapy drugs) for six cycles. You are now on surveillance, which means we are closely monitoring your condition without active treatment.

Your most recent CT scans show that the pancreatic tumor is stable in size (not growing). There are no new concerning findings, and no clear evidence of cancer in other areas. Some liver findings appear to be hemangiomas, which are benign (non-cancerous) blood vessel growths. Your chest scan also does not show any signs of cancer spread.

You recently had an ERCP (a procedure to treat bile duct blockage) because of nausea. During this procedure, doctors removed blockage caused by tumor growth, stones, and clots, and placed a new stent to help bile flow. You are feeling better after this, with improved energy and appetite.

You report some “brain fog”, likely related to chemotherapy, and mild numbness in your feet. These symptoms are noted and will continue to be monitored.

Next steps:

Continue surveillance (close monitoring) Repeat CT scans in about 2 months Follow up with your care team after imaging

Please continue to share any new or worsening symptoms. If you have questions about future treatment options, including clinical trials, we can discuss them when needed.

We encourage you to review this plan with your care team and ask any questions you may have. We are here to support you.

Warmly, Your Care Team

Letter A

Dear Patient,

I want to clearly explain your current condition and care plan.

You have adenocarcinoma of the pancreas, which is a type of pancreatic cancer. Your cancer is stage IV, meaning it has spread outside the pancreas. In your case, it has spread to the abdominal wall (the tissue in your abdomen).

When you were first diagnosed, imaging showed a tumor in the pancreas that involved nearby blood vessels. A biopsy (a tissue sample) confirmed the diagnosis.

You were treated with FOLFIRINOX, which is a combination of chemotherapy medicines used to treat pancreatic cancer. You completed 12 cycles of this treatment. Follow-up scans showed that the cancer responded to treatment and is now stable, meaning it is not growing at this time.

Because the disease is stable, you are currently on a chemotherapy break, also called surveillance, where we monitor the cancer closely without active treatment. Surgery is not recommended because the tumor involves important blood vessels, and radiation is not needed right now since you are not having significant symptoms.

You have had some related health issues:

A blood clot in your leg, which is being treated with medication Thrush (a fungal infection in the mouth), for which you are taking antifungal medicine Some abdominal pain, which has improved with treatment

Your weight and appetite have improved, which is a positive sign.

Next steps:

Continue close monitoring with follow-up CT scans (next planned in November) Watch for symptoms such as bleeding or worsening pain Continue medications for symptom control and prevention of complications Stay in close contact with your care team

Please remember, your care team is here to support both your physical and emotional well-being. If you have any questions or new symptoms, please reach out.

With care and support, Your Care Team

Letter A

Dear Patient,

I want to share a clear summary of your care so far and the next steps.

You were diagnosed with adenocarcinoma of the pancreas, which is a type of cancer that starts in the pancreas. Your cancer was described as borderline resectable/locally advanced, meaning it had grown into nearby blood vessels and was difficult, but still possible, to remove with surgery.

Your treatment so far: You first received chemotherapy (medicine to kill cancer cells) with a regimen called FOLFIRINOX. You completed 12 cycles. After this, you had major surgery to remove the tumor. This included removal of part of the pancreas and nearby structures.

The surgery went well. The pathology report (exam of the removed tissue) showed:

A small remaining tumor (1.7 cm) Most of the cancer cells were destroyed by chemotherapy No cancer found in lymph nodes Clean margins, meaning no cancer was seen at the edges of the removed tissue

Current symptoms: You are recovering from surgery. You have some abdominal discomfort on the right side and have lost some weight, but you are eating and having normal bowel movements.

Recent test results: A recent CT scan (a detailed imaging test) showed:

Small spots in the liver that may represent cancer spread Some changes in the liver that could be from surgery or possibly tumor involvement

Your blood test called CA 19-9 (a tumor marker, which can rise when pancreatic cancer is active) is elevated.

What this means: Your care team is concerned there may be early signs that the cancer has returned, but the spots are very small and not yet certain.

Next steps:

Repeat CT scans in about 2 months Consider a biopsy (taking a small tissue sample) if needed to confirm what the spots are

Please continue to monitor your symptoms and recovery. It is very important to discuss any new symptoms or concerns with your care team.

We are here to support you. Please reach out with any questions.

Letter A

Dear Patient,

I want to share a clear and simple summary of your condition and care plan.

You have been diagnosed with adenocarcinoma of the pancreas, which is a type of cancer that starts in the pancreas. Your tumor is located in the tail of the pancreas. The cancer has grown into nearby areas, which means it is locally advanced (spread to nearby tissues but not distant organs).

What has been found:

A mass in the pancreas that has slightly decreased in size with treatment The tumor is touching nearby structures, including the spleen and adrenal gland There is no sign that the cancer has spread to the chest or lungs A large cystic mass in the pelvis (near the ovary), which doctors think is likely benign (not cancer)

Your blood test called CA 19-9 (a marker that can rise with pancreatic cancer) is within the normal range.

Treatment so far: You have been receiving chemotherapy (medicine to kill cancer cells) with gemcitabine and Abraxane. The schedule was adjusted because of low white blood cell counts, and you are now tolerating treatment better. You have completed 4 cycles so far.

How you are feeling: You report no cancer-related pain. Your appetite comes and goes, but your weight is stable. Your energy improves after each treatment cycle. You have a mild rash, which is manageable.

Next steps:

Continue the current chemotherapy plan Repeat imaging (CT scan) in about 8 weeks to check how the tumor is responding If surgery becomes possible, your team will coordinate removal of both the pancreatic tumor and the pelvic mass

Please continue to share any new symptoms or concerns with your care team. We are here to support you every step of the way.

Sincerely, Your Care Team

Impression and Recommendations:  In summary, Ms. ***** is a 69 y.o. female with a known germline ATM mutation and recurrent metastatic pancreatic adenocarcinoma. She was initially diagnosed in May 2019 with resectable disease and received 1 cycle of FOLFOX prior to Whipple resection (07/09/19) with pT2N2 disease (11/37 lymph nodes), followed by 11 cycles of adjuvant FOLFIRINOX, completed in 01/09/20. She was subsequently diagnosed with biopsy-proven mesenteric nodal recurrence on 09/12/20, and has since received 3 cycles of gemcitabine/nab-paclitaxel with evidence of favorable treatment response to date. She feels entirely well and is holding her own excellently from a functional and nutritional standpoint    We reviewed her diagnosis, pathology, investigations, and treatment, reinforcing why the mainstay of treatment should consist of systemic therapy with the goal of achieving as deep and durable a remission as possible, hopefully translating into significant prolongation of life, but that treatment 
 was not necessarily expected to be curative. As such, it would be important to carefully weigh the risks/benefits of therapy and to prioritize QoL considerations in the process.    We affirmed her treatment thus far, and would recommend continuing on gemcitabine/nab-paclitaxel with an every other week dosing schedule while she is tolerating it adequately, aiming to treat to maximal radiographic/biochemical response or at least 6 cycles in total before considering building in a chemotherapy holiday. She did also inquire about the role for radiation treatment to address any residual disease; I explained that while this can be offered for palliative purposes or to treat isolated recurrences, this modality would not be associated with any clear benefit in terms of prolonging survival.    In terms of future treatment possibilities at a point when her disease progresses, aside from rechallenging with SoC chemotherapy (the combination of 5-FU/LV plus nanoliposomal irinotecan (nal-IRI, or Onivyde) could conceivably be tried, but would likely be of limited yield), we talked about exploring clinical trials which we would be happy to avail her of at *****, although this would very much depend on her overall functional status and goals of care. Possibilities in the future could include:     (1) A study combining nab-paclitaxel and a glucocorticoid receptor antagonist (relacorilant) (includes a pancreas-specific cohort);     (2) A study of pembrolizumab, lenvantinib, and a HIF2a inhibitor (includes a pancreas-specific cohort) -- pending activation;     (3) Other more disease-agnostic trials through our Early Phase Developmental Therapeutics program (e.g. a RAD51 inhibitor, amongst others). There is a trial of an ATR inhibitor for patients with ATM mutations, but there are currently no available slots for patients with pancreas cancer.     Of note, it appears that a FoundationOne panel has been requested for somatic tumor 
 profiling, with results not available at this time although the patient was informed that there were no actionable mutations identified.    All of the patient's questions were answered to her and her family's satisfaction. I am certainly happy to see her again at any point in the future at her request or as clinically indicated.         Time spent:  I spent 60 minutes reviewing the patient's history and medical records in advance, in F2F time during her visit, and in post-visit charting.    Medical decision-making:  Problems: Her active cancer represents a life-threatening illness.  Risk of complications, morbidity/mortality of patient management: High; the patient's systemic cancer therapy requires regular and intensive monitoring for potential major/life-threatening toxicities.

Letter A

Dear Patient,

I want to share a clear and supportive summary of your medical care.

You have pancreatic adenocarcinoma, which is a cancer that starts in the pancreas. You were first diagnosed in 2019. At that time, your cancer was treated with surgery (called a Whipple procedure, which removes part of the pancreas and nearby organs) and chemotherapy (medicine to kill cancer cells).

Your cancer was staged as pT2N2, which means the tumor had grown in the pancreas and had spread to nearby lymph nodes (11 lymph nodes were involved).

After surgery, you completed more chemotherapy. Later, in 2020, tests showed that the cancer had come back and spread to lymph nodes in the abdomen. This is called metastatic disease, meaning the cancer has spread beyond its original location.

Current treatment: You are now receiving chemotherapy with gemcitabine and nab-paclitaxel. These are medicines used to slow or shrink the cancer. Recent scans show that:

The lymph nodes have gotten smaller There are no new areas of cancer seen There is no spread to the chest or lungs

Your blood test called CA 19-9 (a marker that can reflect cancer activity) has decreased over time, which is a good sign that the treatment is working.

How you are feeling: You report no pain and are tolerating treatment well. You have mild fatigue (tiredness) and some nausea, but your appetite is good and your weight is stable. You remain active.

What this means: The current treatment is helping to control the cancer. However, this treatment is not expected to cure the disease. The goal is to keep the cancer under control and help you feel as well as possible.

Next steps:

Continue the current chemotherapy as long as it is working and you tolerate it Repeat scans to monitor the cancer Consider other treatments or clinical trials in the future if needed

Please continue to share any new symptoms or concerns with your care team. We are here to support you every step of the way.

Sincerely, Your Care Team

Next Steps: We will keep giving you these medicines every other week. We will check how you are doing with more tests in the future. If the cancer starts growing again, we will talk about other treatments like joining a research study.

Dear Patient, We hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.

Why did you come to the clinic?

You came in for a consultation regarding your pancreatic cancer.

What's new or changed since your last visit?

You have a poorly differentiated pancreatic ductal adenocarcinoma (cancer that started in gland cells) with lymphovascular invasion. The cancer is now metastatic (pt2n2) and has spread to lymph nodes. Recent imaging shows that the size of the lymph nodes has decreased, indicating a positive response to treatment. Additionally, your CA 19-9 levels, a tumor marker, have decreased, suggesting the treatment is working well.

What treatment or medication changes were made?

You are receiving supportive medication, including morphine (MS Contin) for pain relief. Your treatment plan includes continuing on gemcitabine/nab-paclitaxel (a combination of chemotherapy drugs) with an every other week dosing schedule.

What is the plan going forward?

The goal of your treatment is to manage symptoms and improve quality of life (palliative care). You will continue on gemcitabine/nab-paclitaxel until you have completed at least 6 cycles or until the maximum response is achieved. A genetic test called FoundationOne has been ordered to learn more about your tumor. You will be seen again in person at your request or as needed for further evaluation.

Thank you for trusting us with your care.

Please feel free to contact us if you have any questions.

Sincerely, Your Care Team

Dear Patient,

I want to share a clear and caring summary of your condition and treatment.

You have metastatic pancreatic cancer, which means cancer that started in the pancreas has spread to other parts of the body. In your case, the cancer has mainly spread to the lungs.

Your treatment so far: You have received several types of chemotherapy (medicine used to kill cancer cells), including:

FOLFIRINOX (your first treatment) 5-FU with liposomal irinotecan (second treatment) Currently, gemcitabine and Abraxane

Your current treatment is helping keep the cancer stable, meaning it is not growing significantly.

Current symptoms and side effects:

You have some neuropathy (nerve symptoms like numbness or tingling), which has been getting worse Your abdominal pain has improved You have trouble sleeping You are not having major symptoms like fever, shortness of breath, or severe nausea

Key test results:

Imaging scans show stable disease (no major growth of cancer) Your tumor marker CA 19-9 (a blood test that can reflect cancer activity) has decreased compared to earlier values, though it is still elevated

What this means: Your current treatment is helping control the cancer. However, metastatic pancreatic cancer is not considered curable. The goal of treatment is to slow the cancer and help you feel as well as possible.

Next steps:

Continue current chemotherapy, with a dose adjustment to help with neuropathy Recheck your nerve symptoms in about 2 weeks Repeat CT scans at the next planned time to monitor the cancer Continue medications to help with symptoms like nausea, pain, and sleep

Please keep sharing any new or worsening symptoms with your care team. We are here to support you and adjust your care to keep you as comfortable and active as possible.

Letter A

Dear Patient,

I want to share a clear and supportive summary of your condition and care.

You have pancreatic adenocarcinoma, which is a cancer that starts in the pancreas. Your cancer is described as locally advanced, meaning it has grown into nearby blood vessels and tissues, which makes surgery difficult at this time.

What has been found:

A mass (tumor) in the body of the pancreas The tumor is involving nearby major blood vessels There is no clear evidence that the cancer has spread to distant organs on your recent scans

Treatment so far: You started chemotherapy with FOLFIRINOX (a combination of cancer-fighting medicines). However, you had significant side effects, including:

Diarrhea (loose stools) Low potassium levels Fatigue and poor appetite

Because of this, one of the drugs (irinotecan) was stopped, and your treatment was changed to FOLFOX (a modified chemotherapy regimen). You are now continuing this adjusted treatment and tolerating it better.

Current symptoms:

Your fatigue is improving Appetite is still reduced, with a salty taste in your mouth Weight loss has occurred No current diarrhea No nerve symptoms (such as numbness or tingling)

Key test results:

Recent scans show the tumor is stable (not growing) Blood test called CA 19-9 (a marker that can reflect cancer activity) had increased earlier but is now improving

What this means: The treatment is helping to keep the cancer under control. The goal right now is to continue chemotherapy and see if the tumor can shrink enough to allow future treatment options.

Next steps:

Continue chemotherapy (FOLFOX) Repeat imaging after additional treatment cycles Consider surgery or radiation in the future, depending on how the tumor responds

Please continue to share any new symptoms or concerns with your care team. We are here to support you through your treatment.

Sincerely, Your Care Team

Letter B

Dear Ms. *,

Thank you for your visit today. Here is a summary of our discussion:

Diagnosis and Stage: You have locally advanced pancreatic cancer. This means the cancer is growing in the pancreas and has spread to nearby blood vessels and lymph nodes.

Treatment Plan: We are treating you with a chemotherapy called FOLFOX. This helps control the cancer. We stopped using a part of the treatment called irinotecan because it caused some side effects.

Key Test Results: - Your blood tests show that your white blood cells are high, and your red blood cells are low. This is common with chemotherapy. - Your potassium levels are low, so we need to make sure you get enough potassium. - Your CA 19-9, a marker for pancreatic cancer, has gone up. This can happen even when the cancer is being controlled.

Next Steps: - You will continue to receive FOLFOX chemotherapy. - We will check your potassium levels and give you potassium supplements if needed. - We will do scans after your eighth treatment to see how the cancer is responding. - We will keep checking your CA 19-9 levels each month.

What to Watch For: - Keep an eye on your appetite and try to eat foods you enjoy. - If you have any new symptoms or if your diarrhea gets worse, let us know. - Try to avoid losing more weight. Eating potassium-rich foods like bananas and potatoes can help.

Important Reminders: - Continue to take your medications as prescribed. - If you have any questions or concerns, please talk to your care team.

We are here to support you through this journey. Thank you for trusting us with your care.

Warm regards,

[Your Name] Medical Communication Specialist UCSF Comprehensive Cancer Center

Letter C

Dear Patient, We hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.

Why did you come to the clinic?

You came in for a follow-up visit regarding your pancreatic cancer treatment.

What's new or changed since your last visit?

The CT scan on 12/10/19 shows a stable pancreatic mass measuring with encasement and severe narrowing of celiac and superior mesenteric arteries, without evidence of distant metastases. Additionally, you's clinical status allows continuation of treatment with a chemotherapy regimen, indicating stable disease. However, the CA 19-9 level has increased from 5000 to 11,000 U/mL, which may suggest disease activity despite stable imaging findings.

What treatment or medication changes were made?

You will continue with a chemotherapy regimen only going forward: oxaliplaton and 5FU. Premeds include Aloxi, aprepitant, and dexamethasone. On day 3, you will receive 1L NS, dexamethasone, prn Zofran, and ativan. D3 Fulphila is omitted given ANC >20 today. Potassium management includes + (with 4x Klor Con pills) on D1. Repeat K and Mg on D3: give + (with 4x Klor Con pills) only if K <=3.3. Hold if >3.3. Magnesium: 1g today. Rx for Klor Con: advised to take . Reviewed potassium-rich foods. Consider renal evaluation for RTA if no improvement. Loperamide available prn for diarrhea. Counseled on BRAT diet and avoiding dairy.

What is the plan going forward?

After 4-6 months of upfront chemotherapy, consider either (1) readdressing surgical prospects with your doctor or (2) consolidative chemoradiation, either conventionally fractionated therapy with concurrent capecitabine as a radiosensitizer, or SBRT. Plan for scans again after C8. Monthly CA 19-9 monitoring. Next visit is in-person in 2 weeks.

Thank you for trusting us with your care.

Please feel free to contact us if you have any questions.

Sincerely, Your Care Team

Genomics  Pending STRATA    Impression and Recommendations:  In summary, Mr. ***** is a 52 y.o. male with newly diagnosed metastatic pancreatic cancer.    We discussed the following at today's visit:  I reviewed his imaging, pathology, and staging studies to date, explaining why the mainstay of treatment should consist of systemic therapy (rather than locoregional approaches, such as surgery or radiation), with the goal of achieving as deep and durable a remission as possible, hopefully translating into significant prolongation of life, but that treatment was not expected to be curative.      There are several possible chemotherapy options for metastatic pancreatic cancer, with the choice of therapy depending on a patient's age, performance status, co-morbid conditions, and personal preference. The two current gold standards for front-line treatment, based on phase III data, include FOLFIRINOX (biweekly infusional 5-FU, leucovorin, irinotecan, and oxaliplatin) and the combination of gemcitabine plus nab-paclitaxel. Given his age and performance status, FOLFIRINOX is a reasonable first choice standard of care option.    I also discussed potential clinical trials, notably our ongoing *****#*****: Precision Promise Platform Trial For Metastatic Pancreatic Cancer. Prior to this appointment, he reviewed the sample ICF for this study.      Today, he provided consent to proceed with screening procedures:  The UCSF Experimental Subjects Bill of rights has been 
 reviewed with the patient prior to signing the consent form and a copy has been given to the patient.  Alternative treatment to the patient's disease in lieu of clinical trial participation has been discussed with the patient.  Possible risks and possible side effects of participation in this clinical trial have been discussed.  The patient has been given opportunity to ask questions.  The patient has acknowledged that his/her questions were satisfactorily answered.  The requirement for the use of birth control while participating in this clinical trial was discussed. Letter A

Dear Patient,

Thank you for coming in to discuss your care. I want to summarize what we talked about in a clear way.

You have pancreatic cancer, which is cancer that starts in the pancreas (an organ that helps with digestion and blood sugar control). Based on your tests, the cancer has spread to the liver, which means it is Stage IV.

Your scans showed a mass (tumor) in the pancreas and small spots in the liver. A blood test called CA 19-9 (a marker that can be higher in pancreatic cancer) was also very elevated. These findings support the diagnosis.

Because the cancer has spread, surgery or radiation alone would not be enough. The main treatment is systemic therapy, which means medicine that travels through the whole body to treat cancer wherever it is.

We discussed treatment options. The recommended first treatment is a chemotherapy combination called FOLFIRINOX (a group of cancer-fighting medicines used together). Another option is a different chemotherapy combination. The best choice depends on your overall health and preferences.

We also discussed a clinical trial (a research study) called the Precision Promise trial. You agreed to start the screening process to see if you can join this study. Clinical trials can offer access to new treatments.

You have had symptoms such as weight loss, nausea, and feeling full quickly. You also reported yellowing of the eyes, which may suggest jaundice (a condition where bile builds up in the body). More imaging and lab tests are planned to check this.

Next steps:

Complete additional scans and lab tests Continue clinical trial screening Finalize and start treatment plan

Please remember, your care team is here to support you. If you have any questions or concerns, do not hesitate to reach out.

Sincerely, Your Care Team

Letter B

Dear Mr. *,

Thank you for visiting us at the UCSF Comprehensive Cancer Center. I want to share some important information about your health with you.

Diagnosis and Stage: You have been diagnosed with pancreatic cancer that has spread to your liver. This is called stage IV cancer. It means the cancer has moved beyond where it started.

Treatment Plan: We talked about different ways to treat your cancer. One option is a special medicine called FOLFIRINOX. This is a mix of four different medicines that can help fight the cancer. Another option is a combination of two medicines: gemcitabine and nab-paclitaxel.

We also talked about joining a research study called the Precision Promise trial. This study might offer new treatments that could help you. You agreed to join this study and we will do some tests to see if you qualify.

Key Test Results: - Imaging Tests: We did scans of your belly and chest. These showed a growth in your pancreas and some small spots in your liver. - Blood Tests: Your blood tests showed high levels of a protein called CA 19-9, which can be a sign of pancreatic cancer.

Next Steps: Today, you will have another scan and some blood tests done locally. We will use these results to decide the best way to move forward with your treatment.

What to Watch For: Please watch for any changes in how you feel. Some things to look out for include: - Yellowing of your eyes (jaundice) - Feeling very tired - Changes in your appetite or weight

Patient Identification and Oncology History  ***** ***** is a 74 y.o. male who initially presented to Dr. ***** on 10/30/15 with weight loss (8 lbs over past month), decline in appetite and difficulty in controlling his blood glucose levels.     On 11/02/15, a CT abdomen was obtained which revealed a 7.5x3.5x3.7cm mass in the tail of the pancreas with thrombosis of splenic vein and marked narrowing and encasement of the splenic artery. There was also mass effect on the proximal peroneal vein at the level of the portal confluence. Portal vein and splenic vein were noted to be narrowed but patent. Radiologist report notes that the mass abuts the superior mesenteric artery along its superior superficial margin as it traverses the pancreas. The loss of fat plane between mass and the antral region of the stomach was suspicious for Direction extension.     Subsequently, an EUS was performed on 11/08/15 which revealed the mass extending from the neck to the tail of the pancreas. Pancreatic ductal dilatation was noted. The mass noted to invade the muscularis propria of the stomach (T4 disease) and was noted to encase the splenic vessels. Due to difficulty in advancing the scope into the duodenum, further evaluation of portal vein, lymph nodes and uncinate process was not possible. However, a large lymph node measuring 2.7 cm was noted adjacent to the celiac axis. EUS staging was at least T4, N1. Cytopathology from the FNA of the pancreatic mass was consistent with adenocarcinoma.   He presented to a local urgent care and then ED on 11/12/15 with bilateral lower extremity weakness and slurred speech which lasted for approximately 20 minutes. It was noted that due to the abdominal and back pain he experienced after the biopsy, patient took marijuana  (smoked/ate) after which he was noted to have the symptoms of weakness and slurred speech. He was started on systemic therapy with ***** on 12/05/15.  His restaging scans following 4 cycles of chemotherapy were concerning for metastatic disease that is responding to treatment. His CT chest obtained prior to initiation of chemotherapy had revealed nonspecific pulmonary nodules which decreased in size with chemotherapy raising the concern for metastatic involvement.  as the comparison scans were from October (1 month prior to initiation of chemotherapy). On 05/28/2016, he chose to take a treatment holiday vs beginning clinical trial A Phase 2, Multicenter Study of FOLFIRINOX Followed by Ipilimumab in Combination with Allogeneic GM-CSF Transfected Pancreatic Tumor Vaccine in the Treatment of Metastatic Pancreatic Cancer". Unfortunately, he progressed while on chemotherapy holiday and traveling in ***** as seen on his restaging scans which showed interval disease progression since 05/27/2016, with increased size and infiltration of the pancreatic body/tail mass resulting in new partial bowel obstruction at the ligament of Treitz. New peritoneal and omental nodularity associated with small volume ascites is concerning for progressive peritoneal carcinomatosis; worsening porta hepatis soft tissue infiltration resulting in new biliary obstruction, with moderate right and left-sided intrahepatic biliary ductal dilatation. He underwent ERCP with stent placement by Dr. ***** on 08/24/16.     At this time, his most recent bilirubin was 3.5mg/dL on 08/24/16 and his 08/25/16 CT imaging is most notable for small bowel obstruction. Given these findings, chemotherapy will likely do more harm than good. We therefore discussed the next best step as palliative care. There is a slight possibility of resuming systemic therapy if his obstruction, bilirubin and oral intake improve which is quite unlikely. I briefly discussed the prognosis of the patient's disease with his family. We further discussed the results of his same day xray of his small bowel which was pending at the time. This showed 
 continued blockage despite stent placement which likely represents functional bowel obstruction as opposed to structural obstruction making further stent placement unlikely to help.     Plan, in brief:  - Continue to follow his PO, obstruction, Bilirubin and Liver function  - Follow up on full report of Xray imaging to the small bowel to determine his ability to undergo additional stent placement.  - Anticipate the need to transition the patient to hospice whether he can undergo further stent placement or not.   - Follow up with the patient on the phone directly    I spent a total of 35 minutes face-to-face with the patient's family, over half of that time in direct counseling regarding the patient's cancer diagnosis.

Letter A

Dear Patient and Family,

Thank you for meeting with us. I want to explain the current situation in a clear and caring way.

You have pancreatic adenocarcinoma, which is a type of cancer that starts in the pancreas (an organ that helps with digestion and blood sugar). Over time, the cancer has spread within the abdomen, including the lining of the abdomen (called carcinomatosis, meaning cancer spread on the inner surfaces) and possibly other areas. This means the disease is advanced.

You previously received chemotherapy with a treatment called mFOLFIRINOX (a combination of cancer medicines). The cancer first showed some response to treatment, but later grew again after a break from therapy.

Recent scans show that the tumor is now causing a small bowel obstruction. This means part of the intestine is blocked, which can make it hard for food and fluids to pass through. You had stents placed (small tubes to keep the bowel open), but the blockage is still present.

Your blood tests also show a high bilirubin level (a substance that builds up when the liver or bile ducts are not working well). This suggests biliary obstruction, meaning blockage in the bile drainage system.

Because of these issues—especially the bowel blockage and liver function changes—chemotherapy is not safe at this time and may cause more harm than benefit.

We discussed that the focus of care now should be palliative care, which means care aimed at keeping you comfortable and improving quality of life. There is a small chance that treatment could be reconsidered if your condition improves, but this is unlikely.

Next steps:

Monitor your ability to eat and drink Follow your lab tests, including liver function Review imaging results to guide care decisions Plan for supportive care and possible transition to hospice

We understand this is very difficult news. Please know that your care team is here to support you and your family every step of the way. We encourage you to ask any questions and share your wishes with us.

Sincerely, Your Care Team

Letter B

Dear Mr. *,

I hope this letter finds you well. I wanted to share some important information about your health in a way that’s easy to understand.

Diagnosis and Stage: You have a type of cancer called pancreatic adenocarcinoma. This means there is a tumor in your pancreas. The cancer has spread to other parts of your body, which is why we call it metastatic.

Treatment Plan: We have been treating your cancer with a special medicine called mFOLFIRINOX. Recently, we stopped the treatment because the cancer grew bigger and caused some problems. Now, we are focusing on making you comfortable and managing any pain or other symptoms you might have.

Key Test Results: The latest tests show that the tumor in your pancreas is causing a blockage in your small intestine. This makes it hard for food to pass through normally. We put in special tubes called stents to help open the blockage, but it hasn’t worked as well as we hoped.

Next Steps: Right now, we are watching closely to see how you are doing. We will check your bilirubin levels and how well you can eat. If things don’t get better, we will talk about moving to hospice care. Hospice helps people feel as comfortable as possible when they are very sick.

What to Watch For: Please let us know if you have any new pain, trouble eating, or if you feel very tired. These could be signs that we need to adjust your care.

Remember, you can always ask me or any member of your care team any questions you have. We are here to support you and your family.

Letter A

Dear Patient,

Thank you for coming in for your follow-up visit. I want to summarize your care in a clear and simple way.

You have pancreatic adenocarcinoma, which is a cancer that starts in the pancreas (an organ that helps with digestion and blood sugar). Your cancer is locally advanced, meaning it has grown into nearby areas such as the small intestine (duodenum) and nearby blood vessels, but has not spread to distant organs.

Your scans show that the tumor is about the same size as before, which means the disease is stable (not growing at this time).

You have already received chemotherapy with gemcitabine and capecitabine (medicines that help kill cancer cells). At this point, your team recommends pausing chemotherapy and considering radiation therapy (treatment that uses high-energy rays to target the tumor).

You have had several complications related to the tumor:

Biliary obstruction (blockage of bile flow), treated with a stent (a small tube to keep the duct open) Small bowel obstruction (blockage in the intestine), also treated with a stent Bleeding from the tumor, which has improved

Your blood counts are being checked regularly. If your hemoglobin (the oxygen-carrying part of blood) gets too low, you may need a blood transfusion.

Next steps:

Consider radiation therapy as the next treatment Continue regular blood tests Monitor liver function and stent function Follow a low-residue diet (a diet that is easier to digest and helps reduce bowel blockage) Work on improving nutrition and weight

Your recent tests show no significant increase in tumor size, which is reassuring.

Please continue to work closely with your care team, including your cancer doctors and specialists. If you have any new symptoms or concerns, let them know right away.

We are here to support you every step of the way.

Sincerely, Your Care Team

Letter B

Dear Mr. *,

Thank you for coming to our clinic. Here is a summary of your recent visit:

Diagnosis: You have a type of cancer called pancreatic adenocarcinoma. It is in a part of your pancreas called the head and has grown into the duodenum (the first part of your small intestine).

Treatment Plan: You have been receiving a treatment called Gem/Cape, which is a combination of two medicines. We have paused this treatment for now. We are considering another treatment called radiation therapy to help control the growth of the cancer.

Key Test Results: - Your recent CT scans show that the size of the cancer has stayed the same. - You have a stent in your duodenum and bile duct to help with blockages. - Your blood tests show that your red blood cell count is low, so you might need a blood transfusion if it gets too low.

Next Steps: - We will keep monitoring your condition closely. - You should continue to take your medications as prescribed. - You will need to have regular blood tests to check your red blood cell count. - You should follow a low-residue diet to help with digestion.

Impression and Recommendations:  In summary, Ms. ***** is a 69 y.o. female with a newly diagnosed metastatic adenocarcinoma of pancreatic origin, causing malignant biliary obstruction for which she has just undergone endobiliary stenting. I will also request somatic tumor testing of the patient's tumor specimen, if there is adequate cellularity, through our in-house UCSF500 platform to assess for the presence of any actionable mutations or other genetic alterations that may inform future treatment options, either using already-approved agents or in the context of clinical trials.     We discussed the details of her cancer diagnosis and why the mainstay of treatment in the setting of stage IV (metastatic) disease should consist of systemic therapy (chemotherapy) with the goal of achieving as deep and durable a remission as possible, hopefully translating into significant prolongation of life, but that treatment was not expected to be curative. As such, it would be important to carefully weigh the risks/benefits of therapy and to prioritize QoL considerations in the process.     In terms of standard of care options, there are several possible chemotherapy regimens to select from for metastatic pancreatic cancer, with the choice of therapy depending on the patient's age, performance status, co-morbid conditions, and personal 
 preference; phase III evidence supports either FOLFIRINOX (biweekly infusional 5-FU, leucovorin, irinotecan, and oxaliplatin) or the combination of gemcitabine plus nab-paclitaxel as appropriate front-line choices. In *****. *****'s case, at least based on assessment at today's video visit I would favor going with the combination of gemcitabine and nab-paclitaxel, although I am willing to re-evaluate/reconsider when I have the opportunity to meet her in person.    From the standpoint of clinical trial options for the first-line treatment of metastatic pancreatic cancer, we have the following here at ***** for which the patient could be considered, if interested and eligible (however, these would delay her being able to start on treatment immediately, and thus we are more likely to proceed with SoC chemotherapy):    - Our Precision Promise clinical trial platform, supported by the Pancreatic Cancer Action Network (PanCAN). In the first-line setting, this consists of randomization to either SOC chemotherapy (gemcitabine/nab-paclitaxel or FOLFIRINOX); or an experimental therapy arm that presently consists of gemcitabine/nab-paclitaxel plus pamrevlumab, an anti-CTGF (connective tissue growth factor) monoclonal antibody). Of note, this trial mandates fresh tumor tissue collection both pre- and on-treatment.    - The REVOLUTION (PICI0044) trial, sponsored by the Parker Institute for Cancer Immunotherapy (PICI). This trial also employs a platform design intended to test multiple different chemotherapy/immunotherapy combinations; at present, the one cohort open at ***** consists of the combination of gemcitabine/nab-paclitaxel together with hydroxychloroquine (an anti-autophagy agent) and two doses of low-dose ipilimumab. This trial is non-randomized; and also requires either an adequate amount of archived tumor material or a fresh pre-treatment biopsy for correlative analyses. [CURRENTLY ON HOLD TO ENROLLMENT FOR SAFETY 
 ,PAUSE].    - A phase 1/1b study of TTX-030 (an anti-CD39 antibody) that is being run through our Cancer Immunotherapy Program; this includes a 1st-line pancreas-specific cohort where this agent is being evaluated in combination with gemcitabine/nab-paclitaxel.    All of the patient's questions were answered to her and her family's satisfaction. We will arrange short-term in-person follow up for both clinical and laboratory assessment (including making sure her LFTs have sufficiently normalized) to get her started on chemotherapy ASAP.      Medical decision-making:  Problems: Her active cancer represents a life-threatening illness.  Risk of complications, morbidity/mortality of patient management: High; the patient's systemic cancer therapy requires regular and intensive monitoring for potential major/life-threatening toxicities.

Letter A

Dear Patient,

Thank you for meeting with us. I want to explain your condition and next steps in a clear and supportive way.

You have pancreatic adenocarcinoma, which is a cancer that starts in the pancreas (an organ that helps with digestion and blood sugar). Your cancer has spread to the liver and nearby lymph nodes, which means it is Stage IV (metastatic).

Your scans showed a mass in the pancreas and several spots in the liver. Blood tests showed a high CA 19-9 level, which is a marker that can be elevated in pancreatic cancer. You also developed biliary obstruction (a blockage in the bile ducts), which caused yellowing of the eyes. A stent (a small tube) was placed to help bile flow.

Because the cancer has spread, treatment focuses on systemic therapy, which means medicine that travels through the whole body to treat cancer wherever it is. The goal is to control the cancer, improve symptoms, and help you live longer, but it is not expected to cure the disease.

We discussed treatment options. The recommended plan is to start chemotherapy with gemcitabine and nab-paclitaxel (medicines that work together to slow or shrink cancer). Another option is a different chemotherapy combination, depending on your health and preferences.

We also talked about clinical trials (research studies that test new treatments). These may be an option, but they could delay starting treatment right away.

Next steps:

Follow up in person soon for exam and repeat lab tests Make sure liver function improves after stent placement Start chemotherapy as soon as it is safe to do so Consider clinical trials if interested and eligible

Please know that your care team is here to support you. If you have any questions or concerns, we encourage you to reach out and discuss them with us.

Sincerely, Your Care Team

Letter B

Dear Ms. *,

Thank you for joining me for your video visit today. I want to make sure you understand everything we talked about and what comes next.

Diagnosis and Stage: You have a type of cancer called metastatic pancreatic adenocarcinoma. This means the cancer started in your pancreas and has spread to other parts of your body.

Treatment Plan: The main treatment for your cancer will be chemotherapy. Chemotherapy uses medicines to kill cancer cells. We will start with a combination of two drugs: gemcitabine and nab-paclitaxel. These drugs help slow down the growth of cancer and can improve how you feel.

Key Test Results: We looked at some tests that show how your body is working. Some of your blood tests showed that your liver is having a hard time. We also saw that you have a tumor in your pancreas and some spots in your liver that might be cancer too.

Next Steps: We will set up a time for you to come to the clinic so we can check on you in person. We need to make sure your liver is working better before we start the chemotherapy. Once we see that your liver is okay, we will start the treatment right away.

Letter A

Dear Patient,

I’m writing to summarize your recent visit and to help explain your care plan in clear terms.

You have pancreatic cancer (cancer that starts in the pancreas). Your cancer was treated with surgery, called a Whipple procedure (removal of part of the pancreas and nearby organs). Based on the surgery results, your cancer was stage ypT3N2, which means the tumor had grown outside the pancreas and had spread to nearby lymph nodes (small glands that help fight infection).

Before surgery, you received chemotherapy (medicine to kill cancer cells) with a regimen called FOLFIRINOX. After surgery, the pathology showed that some cancer cells were still present at the edge of the removed tissue (called a positive margin, meaning cancer cells may have been left behind). Several lymph nodes also had cancer.

Recent imaging scans (PET/CT and ultrasound) have not shown clear spread of cancer to other organs. However, your blood tumor marker, CA 19-9 (a protein that can rise when pancreatic cancer is active), has increased. This can be a sign that the cancer may be active again, so we are watching this closely.

Right now, you are feeling well overall, with no pain. Some prior issues, like fluid buildup in the abdomen (ascites), have improved after treatment to relieve pressure in a blood vessel.

Plan and Next Steps: Repeat the CA 19-9 blood test soon Get a CT scan (detailed imaging test) in about 2 weeks Return to clinic after these tests to review results and decide next steps Continue taking Creon (pancreatic enzymes to help digestion) Work on better control of diabetes with your care team

Please remember, this plan may change based on your test results. If you notice any new symptoms, contact your care team right away.

If you have questions or concerns, please bring them to your next visit. Your team is here to support you every step of the way.

Warmly, Your Care Team

Letter B

Dear [Patient Name],

I hope this letter finds you well. I wanted to share some important information about your recent visit and your health.

Diagnosis and Stage: You have a type of cancer called pancreatic cancer. It started in the head part of your pancreas. Your doctors say it is at a stage called pT2N3. This means the cancer has spread to some nearby lymph nodes.

Treatment Plan: We are watching closely because there is a chance the cancer could come back. We will check your blood tests and do imaging tests to keep track of how you are doing.

Key Test Results: Some of your blood tests show that certain numbers are higher than they should be. These numbers help us know if the cancer might be coming back. We will check these again soon.

Next Steps: - We will check your blood tests again this week. - You will have a CT scan in two weeks. - Keep taking your medicine for digestion and diabetes. - If you notice any new symptoms or feel worried, please call us.

What to Watch For: Please let us know if you have any new pain, swelling, or if you feel very tired. Also, keep an eye on your blood sugar levels and make sure they stay within the right range.

Remember, you can always talk to your care team if you have any questions or concerns. We are here to support you.

Best wishes,

Letter C

You came in for a follow-up visit regarding your pancreatic cancer treatment.

What's new or changed since your last visit?

Your recent blood tests showed an increase in a tumor marker called CA 19-9, which suggests the possibility of cancer returning or growing. However, your latest imaging tests did not show any signs of cancer spread or growth.

What treatment or medication changes were made?

Your medication XARELTO (used to prevent blood clots) has been put on hold. You will continue to take Creon to help with digestion.

What is the plan going forward?

You will have your CA 19-9 levels checked again this week. You will also have a CT scan in 2 weeks to monitor your condition. Your next visit will be in 2 weeks to review these results.

Thank you for trusting us with your care.

Please feel free to contact us if you have any questions.

Sincerely, Your Care Team

Letter A

Dear Patient,

I’m writing to summarize your recent visit and explain your care plan in clear terms.

You have pancreatic adenocarcinoma (a cancer that starts in the pancreas). Based on your scans and tests, your cancer is stage IIB, which means the tumor is in the pancreas and has spread to nearby lymph nodes, but not to distant organs.

Because of your overall health and personal preferences, surgery is not recommended. Instead, you are being treated with chemotherapy (medicine to help control cancer) using a drug called gemcitabine. The goal of this treatment is to control symptoms and slow the cancer, but it is not expected to cure the disease.

Your recent CT scans show encouraging results. The pancreatic mass (tumor) is no longer clearly visible, likely due to the effect of treatment. There is also no evidence that the cancer has spread to other organs.

The scans did find two other areas that we will watch closely:

A small spot in the left kidney that could be a different type of growth Small nodules in the lung that may represent another type of tumor

At this time, the main focus remains on treating the pancreatic cancer.

You mentioned low energy, low appetite, and weight loss. These are common during treatment. It is good that you are trying to eat small, frequent meals.

Plan and Next Steps: Continue gemcitabine chemotherapy, now on an every-other-week schedule to improve tolerance Check CA 19-9 blood tests monthly (a marker that can help track cancer activity) Repeat CT scans every 2 months to monitor your response Work with a nutrition specialist to support your weight and strength Continue dental care and mouth rinses as advised

Please contact your care team if you notice any new symptoms or concerns.

You are not alone in this. Your team is here to support you at every step. Be sure to bring any questions to your next visit.

Warmly, Your Care Team

Dear [Patient's Name],

Diagnosis and Stage: You have a type of cancer called pancreatic adenocarcinoma. It is in stage IIB, which means it has spread to nearby lymph nodes but not to other parts of the body.

Treatment Plan: Because of your age and other health conditions, surgery is not a good option for you. Instead, we are using a medicine called gemcitabine to help control the cancer and make you feel better. We recently changed the schedule to give you the medicine every two weeks instead of weekly to make it easier on your body.

Key Test Results: Recent tests show that the tumor in your pancreas has gotten smaller, which is a good sign. There are also some small growths in your lungs and kidney that we will keep an eye on, but right now, they do not seem to be causing any problems.

Next Steps: We will continue giving you the gemcitabine medicine. We will also check your blood and do scans every two months to see how the treatment is working. Your next set of scans will be after this cycle of treatment.

Letter A

Dear Patient,

I’m writing to summarize your recent visit and explain your care plan in clear terms.

You have locally advanced pancreatic adenocarcinoma (a cancer that starts in the pancreas and has grown into nearby blood vessels but has not spread to distant organs).

Your cancer was first found after you had abdominal pain. Imaging scans showed a tumor in the body and tail of the pancreas. A biopsy (a test where a small tissue sample is taken) confirmed this is pancreatic cancer.

You later started chemotherapy (medicine to treat cancer) with a regimen called FOLFIRINOX. You completed 8 cycles of this treatment. Follow-up scans showed that the tumor became slightly smaller and then stable (meaning it was not growing).

At this time, you are on a chemotherapy break (a planned pause in treatment) since November 2018. You are currently feeling well overall. You are staying active and eating well. You do have some intermittent abdominal pain, but it is not getting worse.

Your recent blood tests show improvement in your tumor marker CA 19-9 (a protein in the blood that can reflect cancer activity) compared to earlier values, which is encouraging.

Plan and Next Steps: Continue your chemotherapy break for now Return for a clinic visit in June 2019 Plan for a CT scan (detailed imaging test) in July 2019 to check the cancer Monitor your symptoms, especially any increase in abdominal pain

Please contact your care team if you notice new or worsening symptoms.

You are doing well, and it is important to keep up your activity and nutrition. Your care team will continue to monitor you closely and adjust your plan as needed.

If you have any questions or concerns, please bring them to your next visit. We are here to support you.

Warmly, Your Care Team

Letter B

Dear [Patient Name],

I hope this letter finds you well. I wanted to share some important information from your recent visit with us.

Diagnosis and Stage: You have a type of cancer called pancreatic adenocarcinoma. It is in a stage we call "locally advanced." This means the cancer is growing in the pancreas but has not spread to other parts of your body yet.

Treatment Plan: You have completed 8 cycles of a treatment called FOLFIRINOX. This is a mix of different medicines used to fight cancer. You are now taking a break from chemotherapy. We will check on how you are doing in the future.

Key Test Results: The tests show that the cancer is not growing bigger right now. Your blood tests also look good. You have some mild pain in your upper belly, but it is not getting worse.

Next Steps: We want to see you again in June. At that time, we will do another scan to check on the cancer. If you have any questions or concerns before then, please let us know.

What to Watch For: Keep an eye on any changes in your health. If you notice anything new or if your pain gets worse, please tell us right away.

Remember, you can always talk to your care team about any questions or worries you might have. We are here to help you through this journey.

Letter A

Dear Patient, I’m writing to summarize your recent visit and explain your care plan in clear terms. You were diagnosed with pancreatic adenocarcinoma (a cancer that starts in the pancreas) in the tail of your pancreas. The tumor was about 3.9 cm and had not spread to other organs at the time of diagnosis. You first received chemotherapy (medicine to treat cancer) before surgery, but you had severe side effects, so this treatment was stopped. You then had surgery on 03/01/22, called a distal pancreatectomy and splenectomy (removal of part of the pancreas and the spleen). The pathology showed that the tumor was removed with negative margins (no cancer cells seen at the edges of the removed tissue), but 2 out of 29 lymph nodes had cancer, which means some spread to nearby lymph nodes. After surgery, you started postoperative chemotherapy with gemcitabine and capecitabine. However, you have had significant side effects, including:

Mucositis (painful mouth sores)

Hand-foot syndrome (redness, pain, and peeling of the hands and feet)

Fatigue and balance problems with falls

Because of these symptoms, your care team has decided to pause chemotherapy until your side effects improve. Your most recent CT scans show no evidence of cancer spread in the abdomen or pelvis, which is reassuring. A new finding on your chest scan showed a pulmonary embolism (a blood clot in the lung). This is a serious condition that needs treatment. Plan and Next Steps:

Hold chemotherapy until your symptoms improve

Restart treatment later with a modified schedule if tolerated

Begin blood thinner treatment (Lovenox) for the lung blood clot

Use a walker to prevent falls

Monitor your symptoms closely, especially balance and fatigue

Please contact your care team right away if you have new symptoms, such as worsening shortness of breath, chest pain, or more frequent falls. If you have any questions, please discuss them with your care team. We are here to support you through this. Warmly, Your Care Team

Letter B

Dear Patient,

I hope this letter finds you well. I want to share some important information about your health in a way that is easy to understand.

Diagnosis and Stage: You have a type of cancer called adenocarcinoma. This cancer was found in the tail part of your pancreas. The cancer has spread to two lymph nodes, but it hasn't spread to other parts of your body.

Treatment Plan: You had surgery to remove the cancer from your pancreas. After the surgery, you started chemotherapy to help prevent the cancer from coming back. However, the chemotherapy made you feel very sick, so we stopped it for now.

Key Test Results: Recent tests show that you have a low sodium level in your blood. We are watching this closely. Also, there was a small blood clot found in your lung. We will give you medicine to help prevent more clots.

Next Steps: We will wait until your skin problems from the chemotherapy get better before starting again. We will also check if the medicine is causing you to fall. You are using a walker to stay safe.

What to Watch For: Please watch for any new symptoms like dizziness or trouble walking. If you notice anything different, let us know right away.

Remember, you can always talk to your care team if you have any questions or concerns. We are here to support you.

Warm regards,

[Your Name] Medical Communication Specialist Cancer Center

Letter C

Dear Patient, We hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.

Why did you come to the clinic?

You came in for a new visit regarding your cancer treatment.

What's new or changed since your last visit?

You have a history of adenocarcinoma (cancer that started in gland cells) of the tail of the pancreas, which was treated with surgery. Imaging shows no new signs of cancer spread. However, you have a pulmonary embolism (a blockage in the lung's blood vessels) and low sodium levels.

What treatment or medication changes were made?

We will pause your chemotherapy due to side effects like hand-foot syndrome and mucositis. We will also start you on Lovenox for the pulmonary embolism.

What is the plan going forward?

We will restart your chemotherapy once your hand-foot syndrome improves. We will treat you 5 days out of 7. We will also check for deep vein thrombosis (DVT) with Doppler tests. We understand that this is a challenging time and that managing these health changes can be stressful. We want to reinforce that you are not alone in this process.

Thank you for trusting us with your care.

Please feel free to contact us if you have any questions.

Sincerely, Your Care Team

Original Clinical Note

  This is an independent visit    SUBJECTIVE  This is a very pleasant 69-year-old African-American woman from *****, *****. She has recently relocated to *****. Letter A

Dear Patient,

I’m writing to summarize your recent visit and explain your care plan in clear terms.

You have pancreatic adenocarcinoma (a cancer that starts in the pancreas) located in the head of the pancreas. Your cancer is locally advanced and unresectable, which means it has grown into nearby blood vessels and cannot be safely removed with surgery at this time.

You were first treated with chemotherapy using gemcitabine and Abraxane, but the cancer continued to grow. You then started a different chemotherapy called FOLFIRINOX (a combination of cancer medicines). You have completed 2 cycles so far.

Recently, you have had several symptoms, including:

Fatigue (feeling very tired) Nausea and vomiting Abdominal pain Swelling in your legs Dark-colored urine

Your blood tests show high bilirubin (a substance that builds up when the bile ducts are blocked) and abnormal liver tests. This can happen when the tumor blocks the bile ducts (tubes that carry bile from the liver).

A recent CT scan shows that the tumor has grown and is causing blockage of the bile ducts, leading to swelling of the bile system. There are also signs that the tumor may be pressing on the intestine, which could cause a partial blockage.

Plan and Next Steps: Pause chemotherapy for now Get urgent imaging and evaluation Arrange an ERCP (a procedure to open blocked bile ducts) Monitor for signs of infection, such as fever or chills Go to the emergency room right away if you cannot keep food down or if symptoms worsen

Your leg ultrasound did not show any blood clots, which is reassuring.

Please contact your care team or go to the emergency room if you have fever, chills, worsening pain, or cannot eat or drink.

We understand this is a lot to take in. Your care team is here to support you. Please bring any questions or concerns to your next visit.

Warmly, Your Care Team

Letter B

Dear Patient,

I hope this letter finds you well. I want to share some important information about your health and the next steps in your care.

Diagnosis and Stage: You have a type of cancer called adenocarcinoma in the head of your pancreas. The cancer is advanced and cannot be removed by surgery.

Treatment Plan: You are currently receiving a treatment called FOLFIRINOX. This is a combination of medicines to fight the cancer. We need to check how well the treatment is working.

Key Test Results: Some of your recent tests show that the cancer might be growing again. Your blood tests show high levels of certain substances, which can mean the cancer is causing problems with your bile ducts.

Next Steps: We need to do more tests to see exactly what is happening. You will have a special picture taken of your pancreas and bile ducts. If there are any blockages, we will refer you to a doctor who specializes in digestive issues to help open them up.

What to Watch For: Please watch for any new symptoms like fever, chills, or trouble eating. If you have any of these, please go to the emergency room right away.

Other Symptoms: You have been feeling tired and having some swelling in your legs. We will look into these issues and try to make you feel better.

Medicines: You are taking several medicines to manage your pain and other symptoms. Please keep taking them as directed.

Important Reminders: Remember to talk to your care team if you have any questions or concerns. We are here to support you.

Take care and stay strong.

Warm regards,

Letter C

Dear Patient, We hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.

Why did you come to the clinic?

You came in for a new visit regarding your localized adenocarcinoma (cancer that started in gland cells) of the head of the pancreas.

What's new or changed since your last visit?

The cancer is not responding to treatment. Recent imaging shows the tumor has grown and is causing blockages in your bile ducts. Lab results also show signs of liver issues, including high levels of bilirubin and enzymes.

What treatment or medication changes were made?

Today’s treatment cycle of a chemotherapy combination was postponed because of concerns about disease progression, biliary obstruction, or ascending cholangitis.

What is the plan going forward?

You will need urgent imaging to check for biliary ductal dilatation or disease progression in the liver. A bilateral ultrasound will also be done to rule out deep vein thrombosis (DVT). You will be referred to a gastroenterologist for an ERCP (a procedure to unblock the bile ducts).

Thank you for trusting us with your care.

Please feel free to contact us if you have any questions.

Sincerely, Your Care Team
